###begin article-title 0
###xml 144 145 144 145 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="fn" rid="fn1">*</xref>
###xml 145 147 145 147 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="fn" rid="fn2">S&#8414;</xref>
###xml 147 147 147 147 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="fn" rid="fn3"/>
Functional Significance of the Interaction between the mRNA-binding Protein, Nab2, and the Nuclear Pore-associated Protein, Mlp1, in mRNA Export*Sâƒž
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
###xml 129 146 129 146 <email xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">acorbe2@emory.edu</email>
 To whom correspondence should be addressed: 1510 Clifton Rd., Atlanta, GA 30322. Tel.: 404-727-4546; Fax: 404-727-2738; E-mail: acorbe2@emory.edu.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 0 15 0 15 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Author's Choice</italic>
Author's Choice
###end p 2
###begin p 3
 applies to Author Choice Articles
###end p 3
###begin p 4
###xml 178 202 178 202 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 754 756 754 756 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 883 891 883 891 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 1079 1086 1079 1086 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 1459 1468 1459 1468 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F73D</italic>
###xml 1547 1552 1547 1552 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">MEX67</italic>
###xml 1557 1561 1557 1561 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">YRA1</italic>
###xml 1582 1589 1582 1589 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 178 202 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
Nuclear export of mRNA requires several key mRNA-binding proteins that recognize and remodel the mRNA and target it for export via interactions with the nuclear pore complex. In Saccharomyces cerevisiae, the shuttling heterogeneous nuclear ribonucleoprotein, Nab2, which is essential for mRNA export, specifically recognizes poly(A) RNA and binds to the nuclear pore-associated protein, myosin-like protein 1 (Mlp1), which functions in mRNA export and quality control. Specifically, the N-terminal domain of Nab2 (Nab2-N; residues 1-97) interacts directly with the C-terminal globular domain of Mlp1 (CT-Mlp1: residues 1490-1875). Recent structural and binding studies focused on Nab2-N have shown that Nab2-N contains a hydrophobic patch centered on Phe73 that is critical for interaction with Mlp1. Engineered amino acid changes within this patch disrupt the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction in vitro. Given the importance of Nab2 and Mlp1 to mRNA export, we have examined the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction in greater detail and analyzed the functional consequences of disrupting the interaction in vivo. We find that the Nab2-binding domain of Mlp1 (Mlp1-NBD) maps to a 183-residue region (residues 1586-1768) within CT-Mlp1, binds directly to Nab2 with micromolar affinity, and confers nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA. Furthermore, we show that cells expressing a Nab2 F73D mutant that cannot interact with Mlp1 exhibit nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA and that this nab2 F73D mutant genetically interacts with alleles of two essential mRNA export genes, MEX67 and YRA1. These data provide in vivo evidence for a model of mRNA export in which Nab2 is important for targeting mRNAs to the nuclear pore for export.
###end p 4

###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 117 118 117 118 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref1">1</xref>
###xml 119 120 119 120 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref3">3</xref>
###xml 346 347 346 347 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref4">4</xref>
###xml 490 491 490 491 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="fn" rid="fn4">2</xref>
###xml 753 754 753 754 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref1">1</xref>
###xml 755 756 755 756 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref3">3</xref>
###xml 758 759 758 759 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref5">5</xref>
###xml 915 916 915 916 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref6">6</xref>
###xml 918 919 918 919 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref7">7</xref>
Production of mature mRNA for translation is a complex multistep process involving an array of RNA-binding proteins (1-3). From its transcriptional inception in the nucleus, pre-mRNA that emerges from RNA polymerase II must be bound by processing factors for 5'-end capping, splicing, and 3'-end cleavage and polyadenylation to reach maturation (4). Alongside these processing events, maturing mRNA must be co-transcriptionally loaded with heterogeneous nuclear ribonucleoproteins (hnRNPs),2 mRNA export adaptors, and an mRNA export receptor to facilitate its active export from the nucleus to the cytoplasm through nuclear pore complexes (NPCs), large proteinaceous channels that perforate the nuclear membrane and mediate nucleocytoplasmic transport (1-3, 5). There are a number of nuclear quality controls that safeguard against improperly processed mRNA being exported and translated into deleterious proteins (6, 7). In particular, this quality control machinery likely monitors the state of processed mRNA by checking for the presence or absence of mRNA processing and export factors.
###end p 8
###begin p 9
###xml 192 240 192 240 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Saccharomyces cerevisiae, Caenorhabditis elegans</italic>
###xml 246 269 246 269 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Drosophila melanogaster</italic>
###xml 328 329 328 329 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref8">8</xref>
###xml 330 332 330 332 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref12">12</xref>
###xml 338 351 338 351 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 394 401 394 401 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5</italic>
###xml 492 493 492 493 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref8">8</xref>
###xml 760 762 760 762 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref12">12</xref>
###xml 763 765 763 765 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref17">17</xref>
###xml 192 216 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 218 240 <span type="species:ncbi:6239">Caenorhabditis elegans</span>
###xml 246 269 <span type="species:ncbi:7227">Drosophila melanogaster</span>
###xml 338 351 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
One of the key players in mRNA export is the conserved heterodimeric mRNA export receptor, Mex67/Mtr2 (NXF1/NXT1 or TAP/p15 in metazoans), which plays an essential role in bulk mRNA export in Saccharomyces cerevisiae, Caenorhabditis elegans, and Drosophila melanogaster and stimulates export of transcripts in vertebrate cells (8-12). In S. cerevisiae, a temperature-sensitive mutant of Mex67, mex67-5, shows significant nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA at the non-permissive temperature (8). The Mex67/Mtr2 heterodimer interacts directly with phenylalanine-glycine (FG) repeat-containing nuclear pore proteins (nucleoporins or Nups) in the NPC, which facilitates translocation of the receptor with its mRNA cargo through the transport channel of the pore (12-17).
###end p 9
###begin p 10
###xml 261 263 261 263 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref18">18</xref>
###xml 264 266 264 266 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref20">20</xref>
###xml 272 285 272 285 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 360 366 360 366 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">yra1&#8211;8</italic>
###xml 444 446 444 446 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref18">18</xref>
###xml 448 450 448 450 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref21">21</xref>
###xml 504 506 504 506 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref22">22</xref>
###xml 508 510 508 510 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref23">23</xref>
###xml 586 596 586 596 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Drosophila</italic>
###xml 600 610 600 610 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">C. elegans</italic>
###xml 704 717 704 717 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 719 721 719 721 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref24">24</xref>
###xml 722 724 722 724 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref26">26</xref>
###xml 863 876 863 876 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1020 1022 1020 1022 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref27">27</xref>
###xml 1023 1025 1023 1025 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref31">31</xref>
###xml 272 285 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 600 610 <span type="species:ncbi:6239">C. elegans</span>
###xml 704 717 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 807 812 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 863 876 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 928 933 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
In addition to the Mex67/Mtr2 receptor, efficient mRNA export also requires mRNA adaptors. The most well characterized mRNA adaptor is the evolutionarily conserved protein, Yra1 (Aly/REF in metazoans), which binds to RNA and recruits Mex67/Mtr2 to transcripts (18-20). In S. cerevisiae, Yra1 is essential for mRNA export and thermosensitive alleles, including yra1-8, show nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA at the non-permissive temperature (18, 21). In vertebrates, Aly/REF also mediates mRNA export (22, 23). Interestingly, however, Aly/REF is not essential for bulk mRNA export in Drosophila or C. elegans and Yra1 only binds to about 1000 mRNA transcripts, representing one-fifth of the genome, in S. cerevisiae (24-26). These results suggest that different mRNA adaptors can operate in metazoans and yeast. An example of an alternative mRNA adaptor is the S. cerevisiae hnRNP, Npl3, which is essential for mRNA export in yeast, associates with mRNA co-transcriptionally, and can bind to the Mex67 export protein (27-31).
###end p 10
###begin p 11
###xml 45 58 45 58 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 139 141 139 141 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref32">32</xref>
###xml 142 144 142 144 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref34">34</xref>
###xml 245 247 245 247 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref31">31</xref>
###xml 249 251 249 251 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref32">32</xref>
###xml 253 255 253 255 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref35">35</xref>
###xml 362 364 362 364 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 449 451 449 451 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref37">37</xref>
###xml 552 554 552 554 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref31">31</xref>
###xml 556 558 556 558 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref34">34</xref>
###xml 560 562 560 562 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 642 644 642 644 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref34">34</xref>
###xml 716 723 716 723 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 792 803 792 803 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2-1/nab2</italic>
###xml 809 810 805 806 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 933 935 929 931 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 45 58 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 132 137 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 576 581 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Another potential mRNA export adaptor is the S. cerevisiae hnRNP, Nab2, which is a zinc finger protein essential for mRNA export in yeast (32-34). Nuclear abundant poly(A) RNA-binding protein 2 (Nab2) shuttles between the nucleus and cytoplasm (31, 32, 35) and contains three key functional domains: an N-terminal domain (residues 1-97) involved in mRNA export (36), an RGG domain (residues 201-265) that acts as the nuclear import signal for Nab2 (37), and a C-terminal zinc finger domain (residues 262-477) that specifically binds polyadenosine RNA (31, 34, 36). A putative human orthologue of Nab2, ZC3H14, has a similar domain structure (34). The N-terminal domain of Nab2 (Nab2-N) is critical for Nab2 function in vivo and a Nab2 mutant that lacks the N-terminal domain (residues 4-97), nab2-1/nab2 DeltaN, confers a severe growth defect and nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA when expressed as the sole cellular copy of Nab2 (36).
###end p 11
###begin p 12
###xml 164 177 164 177 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 365 367 365 367 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 368 370 368 370 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref40">40</xref>
###xml 667 669 667 669 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 671 673 671 673 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref40">40</xref>
###xml 674 676 674 676 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref43">43</xref>
###xml 867 869 867 869 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 871 873 871 873 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref43">43</xref>
###xml 874 876 874 876 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref45">45</xref>
###xml 1072 1074 1072 1074 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 1076 1078 1076 1078 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref46">46</xref>
###xml 1193 1195 1193 1195 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref39">39</xref>
###xml 1444 1446 1444 1446 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref39">39</xref>
###xml 1459 1463 1459 1463 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">MLP1</italic>
###xml 1468 1472 1468 1472 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">MLP2</italic>
###xml 1520 1524 1520 1524 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mlp1</italic>
###xml 1533 1537 1529 1533 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mlp2</italic>
###xml 1570 1574 1562 1566 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mlp1</italic>
###xml 1580 1584 1568 1572 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mlp2</italic>
###xml 1665 1667 1649 1651 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref41">41</xref>
###xml 1669 1671 1653 1655 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref47">47</xref>
###xml 1673 1675 1657 1659 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref48">48</xref>
###xml 1699 1703 1683 1687 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">MLP1</italic>
###xml 1888 1890 1872 1874 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref40">40</xref>
###xml 164 177 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1018 1023 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1453 1458 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
For efficient transport of mRNA, export adaptors and hnRNPs may contribute to mRNA export through facilitating interactions with components of the nuclear pore. In S. cerevisiae, the nuclear pore-associated protein, myosin-like protein 1 (Mlp1), acts as a docking platform for hnRNPs and other mRNA factors and also plays a role in mRNA export and quality control (38-40). Mlp1 and its close relative Mlp2 are large proteins attached to the nucleoplasmic face of the nuclear pore that are homologous to the mammalian Tpr (translocated promoter region) protein and are thought to act like control gates at the NPC portal to grant or deny mRNA access to the cytoplasm (38, 40-43). The Mlp1/2/Tpr proteins contain a coiled-coil N-terminal domain that tethers them to the pore via specific nucleoporins and a globular C-terminal domain that binds to mRNA export factors (38, 43-45). Notably, the C-terminal domain of Mlp1 (CT-Mlp1) binds to the hnRNPs, Nab2 and Npl3, and expression of CT-Mlp1 or the C-terminal domain of human Tpr causes nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA (38, 46), linking the Mlp1/Tpr proteins with mRNA export. The Mlp1/2 proteins also bind to the mRNA export adaptor, Yra1 (39). Significantly, Mlp2 binds more strongly to the Yra1-8 export mutant protein than to wild-type Yra1, suggesting that Mlp2 recruits Yra1 for docking of mRNA-protein complexes, but retains malformed complexes that contain the Yra1-8 mutant protein (39). The yeast MLP1 and MLP2 genes are not essential for cell viability and mlp1Delta or mlp2Delta single deletion mutants or mlp1Delta mlp2Delta double deletion mutants are viable and show no significant growth defects (41, 47, 48). However, deletion of MLP1 impairs nuclear retention of intron-containing mRNAs and permits the escape of pre-mRNA to the cytoplasm, supporting the notion that Mlp1 plays a pivotal role in mRNA quality control (40).
###end p 12
###begin p 13
###xml 89 91 89 91 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 288 290 284 286 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 292 294 288 290 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref50">50</xref>
###xml 401 403 397 399 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref50">50</xref>
###xml 455 457 451 453 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 533 535 529 531 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 615 617 611 613 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 663 665 659 661 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 783 785 779 781 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 787 789 783 785 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref51">51</xref>
The N-terminal domain of Nab2 (Nab2-N) is both necessary and sufficient to bind to Mlp1 (49). Structural studies show that this domain has a fold based on a five alpha-helix bundle analogous to the proline-tryptophan-isoleucine (PWI) fold, which is found in several RNA-binding proteins (49, 50). Although the PWI fold is thought to mediate binding to RNA and DNA in other RNA-binding proteins acids (50), Nab2-N does not appear to bind to nucleic acids (49). The Nab2-N structure revealed a hydrophobic patch centered on residue Phe73 that could potentially serve as an interface for protein-protein interactions (49). Previously, we showed that Nab2 residue Phe73 is important for the interaction between Nab2 and Mlp1, but not between Nab2 and Gfd1, another Nab2-binding partner (49, 51).
###end p 13
###begin p 14
###xml 656 663 656 663 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 696 703 696 703 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
Given the way in which Nab2 and Mlp1 function in mRNA export and quality control, we have investigated the interaction between these two proteins in greater detail and show here that the Nab2-binding domain of Mlp1 (Mlp1-NBD) maps to a 183-amino acid region (residues 1586-1768) within CT-Mlp1. The Mlp1-NBD can bind directly to Nab2 and expression of this domain is sufficient to cause nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA, confirming the tight link between Nab2, CT-Mlp1, and poly(A) RNA. We then use a structure-based engineered mutant of Nab2 that cannot interact with Mlp1 to explore the functional consequences of disrupting the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction in vivo. Overall, these studies provide in vivo evidence for a model of mRNA export in which Nab2 is important for targeting mRNAs to the nuclear pore for export.
###end p 14

###begin p 17
###xml 0 7 0 7 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TABLE 1</label>

###begin p 19
###xml 0 26 0 26 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Generation of Nab2 Mutants</italic>
###xml 139 141 139 141 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 157 166 157 166 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F72D</italic>
###xml 181 190 181 190 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F73D</italic>
###xml 349 353 349 353 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
Generation of Nab2 Mutants-Nab2 mutants GST-NT F72D (pAC2349), GST-NT F73D (pAC2350), and GST-NT-F73W (pAC2453) were previously described (49). Nab2 mutants nab2 F72D (pAC2351) and nab2 F73D (pAC2352) were generated by site-directed mutagenesis using oligonucleotides (Integrated DNA Technologies) encoding the F72D or F73D amino acid substitution, NAB2 (pAC717) plasmid template, and QuikChange Site-directed Mutagenesis Kit (Stratagene). All constructs were sequenced to ensure the presence of each desired mutation and the absence of any additional mutations.
###end p 19
###begin p 20
###xml 0 35 0 35 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Protein Expression and Purification</italic>
###xml 127 135 127 135 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 351 367 351 367 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 612 619 612 619 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E. coli</italic>
###xml 860 861 854 855 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 861 864 855 858 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
###xml 896 897 889 890 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 915 916 908 909 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sc>
###xml 1141 1142 1128 1129 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1684 1685 1665 1666 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1941 1942 1922 1923 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1966 1967 1947 1948 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2070 2071 2051 2052 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2201 2202 2182 2183 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2206 2207 2187 2188 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 2209 2210 2190 2191 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 2225 2226 2206 2207 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2237 2238 2218 2219 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2558 2559 2533 2534 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2563 2564 2538 2539 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 2566 2567 2541 2542 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 2582 2583 2557 2558 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2594 2595 2569 2570 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2686 2687 2661 2662 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2691 2692 2666 2667 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 2694 2695 2669 2670 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 2710 2711 2685 2686 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2723 2724 2698 2699 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2787 2788 2762 2763 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2973 2974 2948 2949 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 2978 2979 2953 2954 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 2981 2982 2956 2957 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 2997 2998 2972 2973 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 3009 3010 2984 2985 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 3025 3026 3000 3001 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 3283 3284 3258 3259 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 3288 3289 3263 3264 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 3291 3292 3266 3267 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 3307 3308 3282 3283 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 3320 3321 3295 3296 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 3336 3337 3311 3312 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 3445 3446 3420 3421 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 351 367 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
###xml 612 619 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Protein Expression and Purification-Recombinant GST fusion and His-tagged proteins were expressed in bacteria and purified for in vitro solution and solid phase binding assays. GST (pGEX4T-3), GST-CT-Mlp1 (pAC1340), GST-CT2 (pAC1680), GST-CT4 (pAC1679), GST-CT5 (pAC1678), GST-CT6 (pAC1681), GST-CT7 (pAC1723), and His-Nab2 (pAC785) were expressed in Escherichia coli DE3 cells and purified by batch purification. GST (pGEX4T-1), GST-Nab2-N-WT (pAC2058), GST-Nab2-N-F72D (pAC2349), GST-Nab2-N-F73D (pAC2350), GST-Nab2-N-F73W (pAC2453), His-Nab2-N-WT (pAC2052), and His-S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD (pAC2054) were expressed in E. coli DE3 cells and purified by column chromatography. Overnight cultures were used to inoculate 100 ml of LB media for batch purification or 1 liter of LB media for purification by column chromatography. Cultures were grown at 37 degreesC to an A600 of 0.6-0.8, induced with 200 mum isopropyl 1-thio-d-galactopyranoside, and grown at 30 degreesC for 5 h. For batch purification of GST fusion proteins, cells were collected and lysed in 10 ml of phosphate-buffered saline (PBS) supplemented with protease inhibitor mixture (1 mm phenylmethylsulfonyl fluoride, 3 ng/ml pepstatin A, leupeptin, aproptinin, and chymostatin) by incubation with lysozyme (100 mg/ml) for 30 min on ice and sonication. Lysates were cleared by centrifugation and incubated with glutathione-Sepharose 4B (GE Healthcare) for 2 h at 4degreesC with mixing. The beads were then washed once with 10 ml of PBS supplemented with 0.5% Triton X-100 and twice with 10 ml of PBS. For purification of GST fusion proteins by column chromatography, cells were collected and lysed in 20 ml of buffer A (PBS, 2 mm 2-mercaptoethanol) supplemented with protease inhibitor mixture by a French press and cleared lysates were loaded onto a GSTrap 4B column (GE Healthcare) pre-equilibrated with buffer A. Bound protein was eluted with a linear gradient of buffer B (PBS, 2 mm 2-mercaptoethanol, 10 mm reduced glutathione) and fractions containing GST protein were dialyzed into PBS supplemented with 2 mm dithiothreitol (DTT). For batch purification of Histagged proteins, cells were collected and lysed in 10 ml of lysis buffer (50 mm NaH2PO4, pH 7.4, 300 mm NaCl, 10 mm imidazole) supplemented with protease inhibitor mixture by incubation with lysozyme and sonication. Lysates were cleared by centrifugation and incubated with nickel-nitrilotriacetic acid-agarose (Qiagen) in lysis buffer for 2 h at 4 degreesC with mixing. The beads were then washed twice with 10 ml of wash buffer (50 mm NaH2PO4, pH 7.4, 300 mm NaCl, 20 mm imidazole). His-tagged proteins were eluted from agarose with 1 ml of elution buffer (50 mm NaH2PO4, pH 7.4, 300 mm NaCl, 250 mm imidazole) and buffer exchanged into PBS supplemented with 2 mm DTT using a Centricon centrifugal filter device (Millipore). For purification of His-tagged proteins by column chromatography, cells were collected and lysed in 20 ml of buffer A (50 mm NaH2PO4, pH 7.4, 500 mm NaCl, 20 mm imidazole, 2 mm 2-mercaptoethanol) supplemented with protease inhibitor mixture by a French press and cleared lysates were loaded onto a HiTrap IMAC HP column (GE Healthcare) pre-equilibrated with buffer A. Bound protein was eluted with a linear gradient of buffer B (50 mm NaH2PO4, pH 7.4, 300 mm NaCl, 250 mm imidazole, 2 mm 2-mercaptoethanol) and fractions containing His-tagged protein were dialyzed into PBS supplemented with 2 mm DTT.
###end p 20
###begin p 21
###xml 0 23 0 23 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Solution Binding Assays</italic>
###xml 1155 1157 1140 1142 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 1274 1275 1258 1259 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
Solution Binding Assays-For Nab2 binding to CT-Mlp1 and CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants, Sepharose-bound GST, GST-CT-Mlp1, or GST-CT2 and -4-7 (6 mug) was incubated with 2 mug of purified His-Nab2 at 4 degreesC in 1 ml of PBS supplemented with protease inhibitor mixture, 0.1 mg/ml BSA competitor, and 0.5% Triton X-100 for 1 h 30 min at 4degreesC with mixing. Unbound fractions were collected and the beads were washed three times with 1 ml of PBS for 5 min each. Bound fractions were eluted with loading buffer and samples were analyzed by SDS-PAGE followed immunoblotting with an anti-His antibody (Santa Cruz Biotechnology, Santa Cruz, CA). The intensities of the His-Nab2 bands in the bound fractions were quantitated by integration of the pixel densities of the bands using ImageJ version 1.4 software (National Institute of Health, MD; ) and percentage bound relative to CT-Mlp1 was calculated by dividing the His-Nab2 band intensity in each bound fraction by the His-Nab2 band intensity in the bound fraction of GST-CT-Mlp1. For Mlp1-NBD binding to Nab2-N, His-Nab2-N-WT, or ovalbumin coupled to CNBr-Sepharose beads (30 mul), as described previously (49), was incubated with purified His-Mlp1-NBD (20 mug) in 1 ml of PBS supplemented with protease inhibitor mixture, 2 mm DTT, and 0.5 mg/ml BSA for 1 h 30 min at 4 degreesC with mixing. Unbound fractions were collected and the beads were washed three times with 1 ml of PBS for 5 min each. Bound fractions were eluted with loading buffer and samples were analyzed by SDS-PAGE followed by Coomassie staining. For co-purification of Mlp1-NBD with Nab2-N, 50-ml cultures of DE3 cells expressing GST-Nab2-N or His-Mlp1-NBD were combined, collected, and lysed in 10 ml of PBS supplemented with protease inhibitor mixture by sonication. Lysates were cleared and incubated with glutathione-Sepharose for 2 has described previously for batch purification of GST fusion proteins. The beads were washed three times with 5 ml of PBS for 2 min each. Unbound, bound, and wash samples were analyzed by SDS-PAGE followed by Coomassie staining.
###end p 21
###begin p 22
###xml 0 25 0 25 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Solid Phase Binding Assay</italic>
###xml 104 110 104 110 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">et al.</italic>
###xml 112 114 112 114 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref54">54</xref>
###xml 192 193 191 192 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 324 325 323 324 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 339 340 338 339 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 701 702 679 680 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1137 1138 1115 1116 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1699 1700 1660 1661 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1702 1703 1663 1664 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1705 1706 1666 1667 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 1771 1772 1732 1733 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</sub>
###xml 1897 1898 1858 1859 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 1898 1901 1859 1862 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">450</sub>
###xml 2351 2352 2312 2313 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sub>
###xml 2350 2352 2311 2313 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K<sub>d</sub></italic>
###xml 2437 2438 2398 2399 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 2446 2447 2407 2408 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 2463 2464 2424 2425 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 2464 2467 2425 2428 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">450</sub>
###xml 2589 2590 2550 2551 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 2583 2590 2544 2551 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig4" ref-type="fig">Fig. 4<italic>B</italic></xref>
###xml 2627 2628 2588 2589 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sub>
###xml 2626 2628 2587 2589 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K<sub>d</sub></italic>
###xml 2688 2695 2649 2656 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 1285 1296 <span type="species:ncbi:3704">horseradish</span>
###xml 1492 1503 <span type="species:ncbi:3704">horseradish</span>
Solid Phase Binding Assay-Solid phase binding assays were performed essentially as described by Bayliss et al. (54). Microtiter Immulux HB plates (Dynex) were coated with 100 mul/well of 125 nm GST, GST-Nab2-N (residues 1-97), GST-Nab2-N-F72D, GST-Nab2-N-F73D, or GST-Nab2-N-F73W in coating buffer (PBS supplemented with 2 mm DTT and 0.2 mm phenylmethylsulfonyl fluoride) for 16 h at 4 degreesC on a rocker. After adsorption, plates were washed three times by immersion in PBS and incubated in 100 mul of binding buffer (coating buffer supplemented with 3% BSA and 0.1% Tween 20) overnight at 4 degreesC on a rocker. Binding reactions were carried out for 2 h at 4 degreesC with 100 mul/well of 1-5 mum S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD (residues 1586-1768) protein in binding buffer. After binding, plates were washed three times by immersion in binding buffer without BSA and proteins were cross-linked for 15 min at room temperature by incubation in 1 mg/ml 1-ethyl-3-(3-dimethyl-aminopropyl)carboiimide (Pierce) in the same buffer. The wells were then washed for 20 min in PBS-T (PBS supplemented with 0.2% Tween 20), 10 min with PBS-T containing 100 mm ethanolamine, and finally incubated for 10 min in PBS-T containing 3% BSA. The bound S-tagged proteins were detected by incubation with S-protein-horseradish peroxidase conjugate (Novagen) in coating buffer containing 1% BSA and 0.1% Tween 20 for 1 h at 4degreesC on rocker. The plates were washed three times by immersion in PBS. Then, 100 mul/well of horseradish peroxidase substrate (1-Steptrade mark Turbo TMB (3,3'5,5'-tetramethylbenzidine)-ELISA (Pierce)) was added for 30 min at room temperature and the reaction was stopped by addition of 100 mul of 2 m H2SO4. The absorbance of the samples was measured at 450 nm with an ELX808 Ultra Microplate Reader with KCjunior software (Bio-Tek Instruments, Inc.). For each plate, average absorbance values at A450 were determined for GST and GST-Nab2-N proteins at each S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD concentration from triplicate samples. Background GST absorbance values were subtracted from those of the GST-Nab2 proteins. From three plates, absorbance values for GST-Nab2-N proteins at each S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD concentration were used to generate saturation binding curves by non-linear regression using Prism 4 software (GraphPad Software, Inc.) and calculate the mean apparent Kd. Standard error of the mean (S.E.; standard deviation divided by the square root of n, where n = 3) values of A450 readings from triplicate samples of GST-Nab2-N proteins at each S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD concentration for binding curves in Fig. 4B and S.E. values of triplicate mean Kd values for GST-Nab2-N protein binding to S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD in Table 2 were calculated using Prism 4 software.
###end p 22

###begin p 25
###xml 5 38 1 34 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">RGG-Nab2-GFP Relocalization Assay</italic>
###xml 300 302 292 294 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 540 547 524 531 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT-MLP1</italic>
###xml 559 562 543 546 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT1</italic>
###xml 574 577 558 561 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT2</italic>
###xml 589 592 573 576 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT3</italic>
###xml 604 607 588 591 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT4</italic>
###xml 619 622 603 606 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT5</italic>
###xml 634 642 618 626 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">MLP1-NBD</italic>
###xml 104 109 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP Relocalization Assay-To examine the effect of Mlp1 expression on Nab2 localization in yeast cells, we exploited a previously developed assay where relocalization of a largely cytoplasmic Nab2 mutant fused to GFP, DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP, to the nucleus is monitored upon Mlp1 expression (38). For analysis of the effect of expression of CT-Mlp1, CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants CT1-5 or Mlp1-NBD on the localization of DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP, wild-type W303 cells expressing DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP (pAC1036) and containing galactose-inducible CT-MLP1 (pAC1690), CT1 (pAC1696), CT2 (pAC1695), CT3 (pAC1694), CT4 (pAC1693), CT5 (pAC1692), MLP1-NBD (pAC2075), or vector control (pPS295) were grown in minimal media with 2% glucose overnight at 30 degreesC, transferred to minimal media with 2% galactose, and grown to log phase at 30 degreesC. For all samples, the localization of DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP was examined by direct fluorescence microscopy.
###end p 25
###begin p 26
###xml 0 41 0 41 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fluorescence in Situ Hybridization (FISH)</italic>
###xml 109 111 109 111 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref32">32</xref>
###xml 175 182 175 182 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT-MLP1</italic>
###xml 194 197 194 197 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT1</italic>
###xml 209 212 209 212 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT2</italic>
###xml 224 227 224 227 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT3</italic>
###xml 239 242 239 242 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT4</italic>
###xml 254 257 254 257 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT5</italic>
###xml 269 273 269 273 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">MLP1</italic>
###xml 274 277 274 277 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NBD</italic>
###xml 473 477 461 465 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 492 496 476 480 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 502 509 482 489 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5</italic>
###xml 527 531 507 511 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 542 546 522 526 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2</italic>
###xml 552 553 528 529 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 565 574 541 550 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F72D</italic>
###xml 589 598 565 574 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F73D</italic>
###xml 801 807 771 777 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">et al.</italic>
###xml 809 811 779 781 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref55">55</xref>
Fluorescence in Situ Hybridization (FISH)-The intracellular localization of poly(A) RNA was assayed by FISH (32). Briefly, wild-type W303 cells containing galactose-inducible CT-MLP1 (pAC1690), CT1 (pAC1696), CT2 (pAC1695), CT3 (pAC1694), CT4 (pAC1693), CT5 (pAC1692), MLP1-NBD (pAC2075), or vector control (pPS295) were grown in minimal media with 2% glucose overnight at 30 degreesC, transferred to minimal media with 2% galactose, and grown to log phase at 30 degreesC. NAB2Delta cells or NAB2Delta mex67-5 cells containing NAB2 (pAC717), nab2 DeltaN (pAC1152), nab2 F72D (pAC2351), or nab2 F73D (pAC2352) were grown in minimal media with 2% glucose overnight, transferred to minimal media with 2% glucose, and grown to log phase at 30 degreesC. Cells were prepared as previously described by Wong et al. (55). A digoxigenin-labeled oligo(dT) probe and fluorescein isothiocyanate-conjugated anti-digoxigenin antibody (1:200 dilution, Roche Molecular Biochemicals) were used to localize poly(A) RNA. For all samples, cells were examined by fluorescence microscopy. Cells were also stained with 4',6-diamidino-2-phenylindole-dihydrochloride (DAPI) to detect chromatin and hence the position of the nucleus.
###end p 26
###begin p 27
###xml 106 165 106 165 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2, nab2 F72D, nab2 F73D, mex67-5 NAB2, mex67-5 nab2 F72D</italic>
###xml 171 188 171 188 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5 nab2 F73D</italic>
###xml 970 974 970 974 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 979 983 979 983 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2</italic>
###xml 1013 1025 1013 1025 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5 NAB2</italic>
###xml 1030 1042 1030 1042 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5 nab2</italic>
###xml 1127 1131 1127 1131 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2</italic>
###xml 1165 1169 1165 1169 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 1173 1185 1173 1185 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5 NAB2</italic>
###xml 1265 1269 1265 1269 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2</italic>
###xml 1323 1327 1323 1327 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
To quantitate the percentage of cells processed for FISH that showed nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA, NAB2, nab2 F72D, nab2 F73D, mex67-5 NAB2, mex67-5 nab2 F72D, and mex67-5 nab2 F73D cells probed with oligo(dT) to visualize poly(A) RNA and 10 fields of cells for each strain were imaged. For each field of cells, cells were counted and scored using ImageJ version 1.4 software, and the percentage of cells with nuclear poly(A) RNA was calculated. Cells were scored as showing nuclear poly(A) RNA if the fluorescent signal in the nucleus was greater than the signal in the cytoplasm. The mean percentage of cells with nuclear poly(A) RNA was calculated from the percentages in the 10 fields of cells. Variation in the percentage nuclear poly(A) RNA in each field from the mean was assessed by standard deviation (square root of the mean squared). All scoring of nuclear poly(A) RNA was performed blind to avoid biasing results. In each field of cells, at least 195 NAB2 and nab2 mutant cells and at least 43 mex67-5 NAB2 and mex67-5 nab2 mutant cells were counted. The fold increase in nuclear poly(A) RNA accumulation in nab2 mutant cells relative to that in NAB2 or mex67-5 NAB2 cells was calculated by dividing the mean percentage of nuclear poly(A) RNA in nab2 mutant cells by the mean percentage in corresponding NAB2 wild-type cells.
###end p 27
###begin p 28
###xml 0 10 0 10 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Microscopy</italic>
Microscopy-Cells expressing DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP or processed for FISH were visualized by using filters from Chroma Technology (Brattleboro, VT) and an Olympus BX60 direct fluorescence microscope equipped with a photometric Quantix digital camera from Roper Scientific (Tucson, AZ). All images were captured using IP Lab Spectrum software.
###end p 28

###xml 600 601 568 569 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>

###xml 657 661 625 629 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">LEU2</italic>
###xml 690 691 658 659 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">-</sup>

###xml 1287 1294 1228 1235 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5</italic>

###xml 1395 1399 1328 1332 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2</italic>
###xml 1456 1460 1383 1387 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 1469 1473 1392 1396 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 1479 1486 1398 1405 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">mex67-5</italic>
###xml 1503 1518 1422 1437 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2, nab2 F72D</italic>
###xml 1523 1532 1442 1451 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F73D</italic>
###xml 1553 1557 1472 1476 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2</italic>
###xml 1614 1615 1527 1528 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">-</sup>
###xml 1618 1619 1531 1532 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">-</sup>
###xml 1690 1691 1603 1604 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 1691 1694 1604 1607 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
###xml 1730 1731 1642 1643 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">-</sup>
###xml 1734 1735 1646 1647 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">-</sup>
###xml 1941 1942 1847 1848 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 1942 1945 1848 1851 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
###xml 1989 1990 1895 1896 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</sub>
Nab2 Functional Assay-In vivo functional analysis of Nab2 was carried out through a standard plasmid shuffle assay combined with serial dilution and spotting or growth curves. To generate single mutants of nab2 or double mutants of nab2 and mex67-5 or yra1-8, NAB2Delta cells (ACY427), NAB2Delta mex67-5 cells (NAB2Delta MEX67Delta cells (SWY3601) containing mex67-5 TRP1 plasmid (pRS314-mex67-5)) or NAB2Delta yra1-8 cells (NAB2Delta YRA1Delta cells (FSY2327) containing yra1-8 TRP1 plasmid (pFS2152)) containing NAB2 URA3 maintenance plasmid (pAC636) were transformed with NAB2 (pAC717), nab2 DeltaN (pAC1152), nab2 F72D (pAC2351), or nab2 F73D (pAC2352) LEU2 plasmids and selected on Leu-Ura- minimal media with 2% glucose. Cells were grown overnight at 30 degreesC to saturation in Leu-Ura- minimal media with 2% glucose. Cell concentrations were normalized by A600 and cultures were serially diluted in sterile H2O to obtain approximately10,000, 1000, 100, 10, or 1 cell per 3-mul volume. These dilutions were spotted onto control Leu-Ura- minimal media, where the NAB2 URA3 maintenance plasmid is maintained, or Ura- minimal media containing 5-fluoroorotic acid (5-FOA), which selects for cells that have lost the NAB2 URA3 maintenance plasmid (56). Growth of NAB2Delta, NAB2Delta mex67-5, or NAB2Delta yra1-8 cells harboring NAB2, nab2 DeltaN, nab2 F72D, or nab2 F73D as the sole copy of nab2 was examined at 30 degreesC. For growth curve analysis, NAB2Delta or NAB2Delta mex67-5 cells harboring NAB2, nab2 F72D, or nab2 F73D as the sole copy of nab2 were grown overnight at 30 degreesC to saturation in Leu-Ura- minimal media with 2% glucose. Cell concentrations were normalized by A600, diluted 100-fold in 100 mul of Leu-Ura- minimal media with 2% glucose and added to wells of a MicroWell F96 microtiter plate (Nunc). Samples were loaded in duplicate. Cells in plate wells were grown at 30 degreesC with shaking and absorbance at A600 was measured every 30 min for 40 h in an ELX808 Ultra Microplate Reader with KCjunior software (Bio-Tek Instruments, Inc.). Duplicate sample absorbance for time points were averaged and plotted using Microsoft Excel for Mac 2004 (Microsoft Corp.).
###end p 29
###begin title 30
RESULTS
###end title 30
###begin p 31
###xml 0 55 0 55 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Identification of a Minimal Nab2-binding Region in Mlp1</italic>
###xml 202 210 202 210 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 215 222 215 222 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 306 308 306 308 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 722 723 722 723 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 716 723 716 723 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig1" ref-type="fig">Fig. 1<italic>A</italic></xref>
###xml 836 844 836 844 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 849 856 849 856 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 887 895 887 895 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 1237 1238 1237 1238 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 1231 1238 1231 1238 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig1" ref-type="fig">Fig. 1<italic>B</italic></xref>
Identification of a Minimal Nab2-binding Region in Mlp1-Previous work demonstrated that the globular, acidic C-terminal domain of Mlp1 (CT-Mlp1, residues 1490-1875) interacts with the hnRNP, Nab2, both in vitro and in vivo and that ectopic expression of CT-Mlp1 causes nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA (38). These data indicate that Mlp1 is involved in poly(A) RNA export and suggest that one function of Nab2 is to serve as a molecular link between Mlp1 and poly(A) RNA. To test this model and gain further insight into the molecular basis for the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction, we employed a series of CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants with progressive deletions from the C-terminal end of Mlp1 (truncation mutants CT1-5, see Fig. 1A) to map the region within CT-Mlp1 that interacts directly with Nab2. These truncation mutants were used in both in vitro and in vivo binding assays with Nab2. For in vitro binding, recombinant GST, GST-CT-Mlp1, GST-CT2, CT4, or CT5 bound to glutathione beads was incubated with soluble recombinant His-Nab2 and the bound and unbound fractions were analyzed by immunoblotting with anti-His antibody to detect bound Nab2. The results were quantitated as described under "Experimental Procedures." As shown in Fig. 1B, we found that CT-Mlp1 CT2 (residues 1490-1779) binds to Nab2 with similar affinity (98% bound) as intact CT-Mlp1 (residues 1490-1875), whereas CT4 (residues 1490-1682) binds more weakly to Nab2 (16% bound) compared with CT-Mlp1, and CT5 (residues 1490-1585) shows no binding to Nab2 (1% bound). As a control, GST alone does not bind to Nab2. This result suggests that the Nab2-binding region within CT-Mlp1 lies within residues 1490-1779.
###end p 31
###begin p 32
###xml 387 388 387 388 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 381 388 381 388 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig1" ref-type="fig">Fig. 1<italic>A</italic></xref>
###xml 643 644 643 644 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">C</italic>
###xml 637 644 637 644 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig1" ref-type="fig">Fig. 1<italic>C</italic></xref>
The observation that CT-Mlp1 residues 1490-1779 (CT2) binds to Nab2, but residues 1490-1585 (CT5) does not interact defines the C-terminal end of the minimal Nab2-binding region within CT-Mlp1. To further delineate the N-terminal end of the minimal Nab2-binding region, we generated two additional CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants, CT6 (residues 1586-1779) and CT7 (residues 1677-1779) (Fig. 1A). Recombinant GST, GST-CT-Mlp1, GST-CT6, or GST-CT7 bound to glutathione beads was incubated with soluble recombinant His-Nab2 and bound and unbound fractions were analyzed by immunoblotting with anti-His antibody to detect bound Nab2. As shown in Fig. 1C, we found that CT-Mlp1 mutant CT6 (residues 1586-1779) binds to Nab2 to the same extent (>100% bound) as the intact CT-Mlp1 (residues 1490-1875), whereas CT7 (residues 1677-1779) does not bind to Nab2 (2% bound). These data indicate that the Nab2-binding region within CT-Mlp1 resides within residues 1586-1779.
###end p 32
###begin p 33
###xml 0 9 0 9 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">FIGURE 1.</label>
###xml 9 89 9 89 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Mapping of CT-Mlp1 reveals a Nab2-binding region between residues 1586 and 1779.</bold>
###xml 89 90 89 90 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>

###xml 967 975 967 975 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT6, CT7</italic>
###xml 1062 1066 1062 1066 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Nab2</italic>
###xml 1081 1082 1081 1082 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">U</italic>
###xml 1095 1096 1095 1096 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 1319 1324 1319 1324 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">below</italic>
###xml 1341 1342 1341 1342 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">D</italic>
###xml 1508 1513 1504 1509 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CT1&#8211;5</italic>
###xml 1653 1655 1645 1647 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 1744 1747 1732 1735 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Vec</italic>
###xml 1822 1825 1810 1813 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DIC</italic>
###xml 9 1844 9 1832 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="34"><bold>Mapping of CT-Mlp1 reveals a Nab2-binding region between residues 1586 and 1779.</bold><italic>A</italic>, schematic depicting sizes of CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants (<italic>CT1&#8211;7</italic>) and the Nab2-binding domain of Mlp1 (<italic>Mlp1-NBD</italic>) constructed. <italic>B</italic>, CT-Mlp1 truncation mutant CT2 (residues 1490&#8211;1779) binds to Nab2 <italic>in vitro</italic>, but mutant CT5 (residues 1490&#8211;1585) does not bind to Nab2. Recombinant GST, GST-CT-Mlp1 (<italic>CT-Mlp1</italic>), or the GST-CT-Mlp1 truncation mutant (<italic>CT2, CT4, CT5</italic>) bound to glutathione-Sepharose beads were incubated with recombinant His-tagged Nab2 (<italic>Nab2</italic>) and unbound (<italic>U</italic>) and bound (<italic>B</italic>) fractions were analyzed by immunoblotting with anti-His antibody. The percentage of His-Nab2 band intensity in each bound fraction relative to the His-Nab2 band intensity in the bound fraction of GST-CT-Mlp1 is indicated <italic>below</italic> the immunoblot. <italic>C</italic>, CT-Mlp1 truncation mutant CT6 (residues 1586&#8211;1779) binds to Nab2 <italic>in vitro</italic>. The percentage of His-Nab2 band intensity in each bound fraction relative to the His-Nab2 band intensity in the bound fraction of GST-CT-Mlp1 is indicated <italic>below</italic> the immunoblot. <italic>D</italic>, expression of CT-Mlp1 truncation mutant CT2 (residues 1490&#8211;1779) causes relocalization of &#916;RGG-Nab2-GFP to the nucleus. CT-Mlp1 and CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants (<italic>CT1&#8211;5</italic>) were expressed in yeast cells expressing a Nab2 mutant fused to GFP (&#916;RGG-Nab2-GFP), which displays localization throughout the cell (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>), and &#916;RGG-Nab2-GFP was visualized by direct fluorescence microscopy. Empty vector (<italic>Vec</italic>) was used as a control. Corresponding differential interference contrast (<italic>DIC</italic>) images are shown.</p>

###begin p 35
###xml 20 27 20 27 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 62 70 62 70 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 255 262 251 258 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 264 266 260 262 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 350 352 346 348 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref35">35</xref>
###xml 467 469 459 461 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 544 546 536 538 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref37">37</xref>
###xml 548 550 540 542 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref57">57</xref>
###xml 608 610 600 602 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 744 746 728 730 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 859 866 839 846 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 894 902 874 882 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 927 934 907 914 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 1044 1051 1024 1031 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">GAL1&#8211;10</italic>
###xml 1275 1277 1255 1257 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref58">58</xref>
###xml 1642 1643 1614 1615 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">D</italic>
###xml 1636 1643 1608 1615 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig1" ref-type="fig">Fig. 1<italic>D</italic></xref>
###xml 2213 2220 2173 2180 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 732 737 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1011 1016 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1223 1238 <span type="species:ncbi:10633">simian virus 40</span>
###xml 1240 1244 <span type="species:ncbi:10633?0.9966199903428296">SV40</span>
###xml 1290 1295 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
We used a secondary in vivo Nab2 binding assay to confirm the in vitro data mapping the Nab2-binding region of Mlp1 to residues 1586-1779. Previously, we developed a DeltaRGG-Nab2 relocalization assay that reports on the interaction between Nab2 and Mlp1 in vivo (38). In contrast to wild-type Nab2, which shows nuclear localization at steady-state (35), DeltaRGG-Nab2 shows diffuse nuclear and cytoplasmic localization at steady-state due to lack of the RGG domain (36), which is required for interaction with the Nab2 import receptor Kap104 (37, 57). Expression of CT-Mlp1, which localizes to the nucleus (38), causes nuclear relocalization of DeltaRGG-Nab2 fused to GFP (DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP), which normally locates throughout the yeast cell (36). CT-Mlp1-induced relocalization of DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP to the nucleus is consistent with CT-Mlp1 binding to Nab2 in vivo. Therefore, to confirm the in vitro binding data using this in vivo binding assay, we expressed CT-Mlp1 and CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants CT1-5 in yeast from a galactose-inducible GAL1-10 promoter. To ensure nuclear targeting of each of the CT-Mlp1 deletions, the CT1-5 sequences were fused to an engineered bipartite nuclear localization signal based on the simian virus 40 (SV40) nuclear localization signal (58). Wild-type yeast cells expressing DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP were induced to express CT-Mlp1 and CT1-5 by the addition of galactose and the localization of DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP was assessed by direct fluorescence microscopy. The expression and localization of CT-Mlp1 and CT1-5 were confirmed by immunoblotting and GFP fusion localization (data not shown). As shown in Fig. 1D, CT-Mlp1 truncation mutant CT1 (residues 1490-1811) and CT2 (residues 1490-1779) relocalize DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP to the nucleus to a similar degree as the intact CT-Mlp1 (residues 1490-1875). Truncation mutants CT3 (residues 1490-1717) and CT4 (residues 1490-1682) partially relocalize DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP to the nucleus compared with CT-Mlp1 and truncation mutant CT5 (residues 1490-1585) does not relocalize DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP to the nucleus. This result indicates that CT-Mlp1 residues 1490-1779 binds to Nab2, whereas CT-Mlp1 residues 1490-1585 do not interact with Nab2 in vivo. Taken together, the Nab2 binding assays strongly suggest the Nab2-binding region within CT-Mlp1 maps to residues 1586-1779.
###end p 35
###begin p 36
###xml 0 74 0 74 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">The Nab2-binding Region of Mlp1 Causes Nuclear Accumulation of Poly(A) RNA</italic>
###xml 162 164 162 164 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 986 992 986 992 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig2" ref-type="fig">Fig. 2</xref>
###xml 360 365 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 413 418 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
The Nab2-binding Region of Mlp1 Causes Nuclear Accumulation of Poly(A) RNA-Expression of CT-Mlp1 (residues 1490-1875) causes nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA (38). To map the region within CT-Mlp1 that causes poly(A) RNA accumulation and assess whether it corresponds to the Nab2-binding region, we expressed CT-Mlp1 and CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants CT1-5 in yeast from a galactose-inducible promoter. Wild-type yeast cells were induced to express CT-Mlp1 and CT1-5 by the addition of galactose and samples were processed for FISH using an oligo(dT) probe to visualize bulk poly(A) RNA. CT-Mlp1 truncation mutants CT1 (residues 1490-1811) and CT2 (residues 1490-1779) cause nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA to a similar extent as the intact CT-Mlp1 (residues 1490-1875), CT3 (residues 1490-1717) causes partial nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA compared with CT-Mlp1, and CT4 (residues 1490-1682) and CT5 (residues 1490-1585) do not cause poly(A) RNA accumulation in the nucleus (Fig. 2). This result indicates that the region within CT-Mlp1 that is sufficient to cause nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA maps to residues 1490-1779. Thus, both the Nab2-binding and poly(A) RNA interacting regions of CT-Mlp1 reside within residues 1490-1779.
###end p 36
###begin p 37
###xml 0 54 0 54 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Delineation of the Mlp1-Nab2 Binding Domain (Mlp1-NBD)</italic>
###xml 195 203 195 203 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 598 599 598 599 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 592 599 592 599 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig1" ref-type="fig">Fig. 1<italic>A</italic></xref>
Delineation of the Mlp1-Nab2 Binding Domain (Mlp1-NBD)-As a complement to our mapping using engineered deletions, we also isolated a proteolytic fragment of the intact CT-Mlp1 that bound to Nab2 in vitro and identified the Mlp1 tryptic peptides within this fragment by mass spectroscopy (see supplemental Fig. S1 and supplemental Table 1). We mapped these peptides within CT-Mlp1 and used the information to refine the C-terminal end of the Nab2-binding region of Mlp1 to residue 1768. We termed the final Nab2-binding region of Mlp1: Mlp1-Nab2-binding domain (Mlp1-NBD; residues 1586-1768) (Fig. 1A).
###end p 37

###begin p 40
###xml 0 54 0 54 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">The Mlp1-NBD Interacts with the Nab2 N-terminal Domain</italic>
###xml 214 222 214 222 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 224 226 224 226 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 379 387 379 387 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 681 682 681 682 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 675 682 675 682 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig3" ref-type="fig">Fig. 3<italic>A</italic></xref>
###xml 948 955 948 955 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E. coli</italic>
###xml 984 991 984 991 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E. coli</italic>
###xml 1292 1293 1292 1293 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 1286 1293 1286 1293 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig3" ref-type="fig">Fig. 3<italic>B</italic></xref>
###xml 1680 1688 1680 1688 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 1690 1692 1690 1692 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
###xml 1694 1696 1694 1696 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 948 955 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 984 991 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 1547 1552 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
The Mlp1-NBD Interacts with the Nab2 N-terminal Domain-Recent domain analysis of the hnRNP Nab2 has established that the N-terminal domain of Nab2 (Nab2-N, residues 1-97) binds to both CT-Mlp1 and full-length Mlp1 in vitro (49). To determine whether the Nab2-binding domain of Mlp1 (Mlp1-NBD, residues 1586-1768) interacts directly with Nab2-N, we analyzed the interaction by an in vitro binding assay using purified recombinant proteins. A control protein, ovalbumin, or recombinant His-Nab2-N-WT (residues 1-97) coupled to Sepharose beads was incubated with soluble recombinant His-Mlp1-NBD and bound fractions were analyzed by SDS-PAGE and Coomassie staining. As shown in Fig. 3A, Mlp1-NBD binds to Nab2-N-WT, but does not bind to control ovalbumin beads. To further support this direct interaction and test its specificity, we tested whether Mlp1-NBD can be co-purified with Nab2-N from a complex protein mixture. We combined cell lysates from E. coli expressing His-Mlp1-NBD and E. coli expressing GST-Nab2-N, incubated the mixed lysates with glutathione beads and analyzed the bound fraction by SDS-PAGE and Coomassie staining. We find that Mlp1-NBD does indeed co-purify with Nab2-N from a complex mixture of bacterial proteins, highlighting the specificity of this interaction (Fig. 3B). Together, the experiments demonstrate that the Nab2-binding domain of Mlp1 (residues 1586-1768) interacts directly with the N-terminal domain of Nab2 (residues 1-97). Importantly, previous studies show that the interaction between CT-Mlp1 and Nab2 in yeast lysate is insensitive to RNase treatment and therefore RNA-independent and also that Nab2-N does not interact with poly(A) RNA in vitro (38, 49). The direct interaction between Nab2-N and Mlp1-NBD is thus most likely RNA-independent as well.
###end p 40

###begin p 43
###xml 182 189 182 189 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 498 499 490 491 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">C</italic>
###xml 492 499 484 491 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig3" ref-type="fig">Fig. 3<italic>C</italic></xref>
###xml 829 830 821 822 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">D</italic>
###xml 823 830 815 822 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig3" ref-type="fig">Fig. 3<italic>D</italic></xref>
###xml 1189 1196 1181 1188 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vivo</italic>
###xml 611 616 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
To further compare the Mlp1-NBD (residues 1586-1768) with the intact CT-Mlp1, we asked whether the Mlp1-NBD can both interact with Nab2 and cause nuclear accumulation of poly(A) RNA in vivo. In the DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP relocalization assay, we expressed nuclear-targeted CT-Mlp1 and Mlp1-NBD from a galactose-inducible promoter and found that Mlp1-NBD (residues 1586-1768) relocalizes most DeltaRGG-Nab2-GFP to the nucleus, although not quite to the same extent as CT-Mlp1 (residues 1490-1875) (Fig. 3C). To test if the expression of Mlp1-NBD affects poly(A) RNA localization, we expressed Mlp1-NBD and CT-Mlp1 in yeast cells and localized the poly(A) RNA by FISH with an oligo d(T) probe. We find that the Mlp1-NBD does induce accumulation of poly(A) RNA within the nucleus, although not quite to the same degree as CT-Mlp1 (Fig. 3D). These results indicate that the Mlp1-NBD (residues 1586-1768) within CT-Mlp1 is primarily responsible for CT-Mlp1-induced nuclear poly(A) RNA accumulation, but that small contributions are also made by other residues outside this region of CT-Mlp1. Overall, these data confirm that the Mlp1-NBD, like the intact CT-Mlp1, has the capacity both to bind Nab2 in vivo and to impact poly(A) RNA localization.
###end p 43
###begin p 44
###xml 242 244 242 244 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref54">54</xref>
###xml 338 339 338 339 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sub>
###xml 337 339 337 339 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K<sub>d</sub></italic>
###xml 448 449 448 449 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sub>
###xml 447 449 447 449 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K<sub>d</sub></italic>
###xml 480 481 465 466 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 489 490 474 475 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 483 490 468 475 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig4" ref-type="fig">Fig. 4<italic>B</italic></xref>
###xml 492 499 477 484 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
To measure quantitatively the interaction between the Mlp1-NBD and Nab2-N, we examined the binding of S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD to GST-Nab2-N wild-type (WT) at increasing concentrations of S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD in a solid-phase microtiter plate binding assay (54). This analysis generated a saturation binding curve and an apparent dissociation constant (Kd) value for Mlp1-NBD binding to Nab2-N-WT. We find that Mlp1-NBD binds to wild-type Nab2-N with an apparent Kd of approximately1.3 +/- 0.4 mum (Fig. 4B, Table 2), indicating that the relative binding affinity between Mlp1-NBD and Nab2-N is in the micromolar range. The rather weak affinity between Mlp1 and Nab2 likely reflects the need for the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction to be transient during mRNA export.
###end p 44
###begin p 45
###xml 0 73 0 73 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Specific Amino Acid Substitutions Disrupt the Nab2-N/Mlp1-NBD Interaction</italic>
###xml 236 238 232 234 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 306 308 302 304 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 316 317 312 313 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 310 317 306 313 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig4" ref-type="fig">Fig. 4<italic>A</italic></xref>
###xml 404 406 400 402 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 563 571 559 567 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">in vitro</italic>
###xml 652 654 648 650 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">72</sup>
###xml 661 663 657 659 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 1057 1058 1053 1054 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sub>
###xml 1056 1058 1052 1054 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K<sub>d</sub></italic>
###xml 1087 1088 1068 1069 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1121 1122 1102 1103 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 1115 1122 1096 1103 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig4" ref-type="fig">Fig. 4<italic>B</italic></xref>
###xml 1124 1131 1105 1112 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 1222 1223 1203 1204 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sub>
###xml 1221 1223 1202 1204 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K<sub>d</sub></italic>
###xml 1252 1253 1218 1219 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">m</sc>
###xml 1293 1294 1259 1260 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 1287 1294 1253 1260 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig4" ref-type="fig">Fig. 4<italic>B</italic></xref>
###xml 1296 1303 1262 1269 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 1358 1360 1324 1326 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 1488 1490 1454 1456 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 1733 1735 1699 1701 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
Specific Amino Acid Substitutions Disrupt the Nab2-N/Mlp1-NBD Interaction-The crystal structure of the N-terminal domain of Nab2 (Nab2-N, residues 1-97) shows that this domain is based on a five alpha-helix bundle with a PWI-like fold (49). A Nab2 hydrophobic patch centered on a phenylalanine residue, Phe73 (Fig. 4A), was proposed to be an important constituent of the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction interface (49). Consistent with this hypothesis, we showed that recombinant Nab2-N-F73D does not bind to Mlp1, whereas Nab2-N-F72D is still competent to bind Mlp1, in an in vitro bead binding assay. To assess the impact of amino acid substitutions at Nab2 Phe72 or Phe73 on the relative binding affinity of Nab2 for Mlp1, we measured the binding of S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD to GST-Nab2-N mutants F72D, F73D, or F73W at increasing concentrations of S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD in the solid-phase binding assay. We find that the interaction between Nab2-N-F73D and Mlp1-NBD is undetectable in this assay, whereas Mlp1-NBD binds to Nab2-N-F72D with roughly 2-fold lower relative affinity (Kd approximately 2.3 +/- 1.1 mum) compared with wild-type (Fig. 4B, Table 2). Furthermore, we find that Mlp1-NBD binds to Nab2-N-F73W with 1.5-fold higher affinity (Kd approximately 0.8 +/- 0.2 mum) compared with wild-type Nab2-N (Fig. 4B, Table 2). The data reveal that substituting Nab2-N residue Phe73 with Asp significantly reduces the relative binding affinity between Nab2-N and the Mlp1-NBD, supporting the idea that Nab2 Phe73 is critical for interaction with Mlp1. The impaired binding of Nab2-N to Mlp1-NBD seen with the hydrophilic substitution, F73D, together with the enhanced binding seen with the hydrophobic substitution, F73W, is consistent with the proposal (49) that hydrophobic interactions contribute significantly to the Nab2/Mlp1 interface.
###end p 45

FIGURE 4.The Nab2-binding domain of Mlp1 binds to the N-terminal domain of Nab2 with micromolar affinity and Nab2 F73D substitution disrupts the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction.A, surface representation of the N-terminal domain of Nab2 (Nab2-N) showing the putative Mlp1 interaction site (gray) identified by Grant et al. (49). Phe73 (dark gray) is central to this site and its hydrophobic aromatic side chain is completely exposed to the solvent. B, the binding affinity of the Nab2-binding domain of Mlp1 (Mlp1-NBD; residues 1586-1768) for Nab2-N wild-type and mutants F72D, F73D, and F73W (residues 1-97) was measured quantitatively in a solid-phase binding assay (54). Binding of S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD (Mlp1-NBD) to GST-Nab2-N wild-type (Nab2-N-WT), GST-Nab2-N-F72D (Nab2-N-F72D), GST-Nab2-N-F73D (Nab2-N-F73D), or GST-Nab2-N-F73W (Nab2-N-F73W) at increasing concentrations of S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD was measured at optical density of 450 nm (A450) as described under "Experimental Procedures." Measurements at A450 (amount bound) were used to generate saturation binding curves by non-linear regression. Standard error bars for A450 readings from triplicate samples of GST-Nab2-N-WT and mutants at each S-Tag-Mlp1-NBD concentration are indicated. These binding curves were used to calculate apparent dissociation constants for binding between Mlp1-NBD and Nab2-N wild-type (Kd approximately 1.3 +/- 0.4 mum), Nab2-N-F72D (Kd approximately 2.3 +/- 1.1 mum), or Nab2-N-F73W (Kd approximately 0.8 +/- 0.2 mum) (see Table 2). The weak binding between Mlp1-NBD and Nab2-N-F73D was indistinguishable from nonspecific binding, indicating that the F73D substitution essentially disrupts the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction.

###xml 0 52 0 52 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Functional Significance of the Nab2/Mlp1 Interaction</italic>

###xml 1285 1289 1273 1277 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2</italic>
###xml 1295 1296 1279 1280 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 1351 1360 1335 1344 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F72D</italic>
###xml 1365 1374 1349 1358 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">nab2 F73D</italic>
###xml 1409 1413 1393 1397 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">NAB2</italic>
###xml 1557 1558 1541 1542 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 1551 1558 1535 1542 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig5" ref-type="fig">Fig. 5<italic>B</italic></xref>
###xml 1617 1619 1601 1603 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">73</sup>
###xml 1896 1898 1880 1882 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref49">49</xref>
###xml 1900 1902 1884 1886 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref51">51</xref>
###xml 445 450 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 756 761 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Functional Significance of the Nab2/Mlp1 Interaction-With the knowledge gained from the Nab2-N structure and engineered Nab2 mutants, we proceeded to assess the functional consequence of disrupting the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction in vivo and define the way in which this interaction contributes to mRNA export. As the NAB2 gene is essential for viability (35), we examined whether Nab2-F73D or Nab2-F72D (as a control) could replace wild-type Nab2 in yeast cells. As a further control in these assays, we used a previously characterized Nab2 mutant, DeltaN, lacking the Nab2 N-terminal domain altogether (residues 4-97), which confers a severe slow growth phenotype when expressed as the only cellular copy of Nab2 (36). Using a plasmid shuffle assay, NAB2Delta yeast cells maintained by a NAB2 URA3 plasmid and also containing NAB2, nab2 DeltaN, F72D or F73D LEU2 test plasmids were grown to saturation, serially diluted in 10-fold dilutions, and spotted on control plates (where the NAB2 URA3 maintenance plasmid is retained) and plates containing 5-FOA to remove the NAB2 URA3 maintenance plasmid and leave the test plasmids as the sole cellular copies of Nab2. Both nab2 F72D and nab2 F73D cells grow similarly to wild-type NAB2 cells on 5-FOA plates (Fig. 5A). However, as anticipated, nab2 DeltaN cells show a slow growth phenotype. We confirmed that nab2 F72D and nab2 F73D cells grow similarly to wild-type NAB2 cells by measuring the optical density of the cells in liquid culture over time and generating quantitative growth curves for the cells (Fig. 5B). This result indicates that substituting Nab2 residue Phe73 with Asp and disrupting the Nab2/Mlp1 interaction does not significantly affect cell growth in a wild-type background. Additionally, given the knowledge that the N-terminal domain of Nab2 is required for proper Nab2 function (including binding to other proteins such as Gfd1 (49, 51)), the data suggest that the F73D substitution does not severely impact the overall structure of the Nab2 protein.
###end p 48
